<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41598"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Gossypium barbadense genome sequence provides insight into the evolution of extra-long staple fiber and specialized metabolites"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Scientific Reports 2015 5:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2015-09-30"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2015 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Of the two cultivated species of allopolyploid cotton, Gossypium barbadense produces extra-long fibers for the production of superior textiles. We sequenced its genome (AD)2 and performed a comparative analysis. We identified three bursts of retrotransposons from 20 million years ago (Mya) and a genome-wide uneven pseudogenization peak at 11&#8211;20 Mya, which likely contributed to genomic divergences. Among the 2,483 genes preferentially expressed in fiber, a cell elongation regulator, PRE1, is strikingly At biased and fiber specific, echoing the A-genome origin of spinnable fiber. The expansion of the PRE members implies a genetic factor that underlies fiber elongation. Mature cotton fiber consists of nearly pure cellulose. G. barbadense and G. hirsutum contain 29 and 30 cellulose synthase (CesA) genes, respectively; whereas most of these genes (&gt;25) are expressed in fiber, genes for secondary cell wall biosynthesis exhibited a delayed and higher degree of up-regulation in G. barbadense compared with G. hirsutum, conferring an extended elongation stage and highly active secondary wall deposition during extra-long fiber development. The rapid diversification of sesquiterpene synthase genes in the gossypol pathway exemplifies the chemical diversity of lineage-specific secondary metabolites. The G. barbadense genome advances our understanding of allopolyploidy, which will help improve cotton fiber quality."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Of the two cultivated species of allopolyploid cotton, <i>Gossypium barbadense</i> produces extra-long fibers for the production of superior textiles. We sequenced its genome (AD)<sub>2</sub> and performed a comparative analysis. We identified three bursts of retrotransposons from 20 million years ago (Mya) and a genome-wide uneven pseudogenization peak at 11–20 Mya, which likely contributed to genomic divergences. Among the 2,483 genes preferentially expressed in fiber, a cell elongation regulator, <i>PRE1</i>, is strikingly A<sub>t</sub> biased and fiber specific, echoing the A-genome origin of spinnable fiber. The expansion of the PRE members implies a genetic factor that underlies fiber elongation. Mature cotton fiber consists of nearly pure cellulose. <i>G. barbadense</i> and <i>G. hirsutum</i> contain 29 and 30 cellulose synthase (CesA) genes, respectively; whereas most of these genes (&gt;25) are expressed in fiber, genes for secondary cell wall biosynthesis exhibited a delayed and higher degree of up-regulation in <i>G. barbadense</i> compared with <i>G. hirsutum</i>, conferring an extended elongation stage and highly active secondary wall deposition during extra-long fiber development. The rapid diversification of sesquiterpene synthase genes in the gossypol pathway exemplifies the chemical diversity of lineage-specific secondary metabolites. The <i>G. barbadense</i> genome advances our understanding of allopolyploidy, which will help improve cotton fiber quality.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Whole-genome duplication (WGD) or polyploidy is a primary driving force in the evolution of many eukaryotic organisms, especially flowering plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Soltis, D. E., Visger, C. J. &amp; Soltis, P. S. The polyploidy revolution then and now: Stebbins revisited. Am J Bot. 101, 1057–1078 (2014)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d27138e1082">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Hegarty, M. J. &amp; Hiscock, S. J. Genomic clues to the evolutionary success of polyploid plants. Curr Biol. 18, R435–444 (2008)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d27138e1085">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Otto, S. P. The evolutionary consequences of polyploidy. Cell. 131, 452–462 (2007)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d27138e1088">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Ancestral polyploidy in seed plants and angiosperms. Nature. 473, 97–100 (2011)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d27138e1091">4</a></sup>. Many crops are neo-allopolyploids that harbor different sets of genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Renny-Byfield, S. &amp; Wendel, J. F. Doubling down on genomes: polyploidy and crop plants. Am J Bot. 101, 1711–1725 (2014)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d27138e1095">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Gong, L., Kakrana, A., Arikit, S., Meyers, B. C. &amp; Wendel, J. F. Composition and expression of conserved microRNA genes in diploid cotton (Gossypium) species. Genome biol Evol. 5, 2449–2459 (2013)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d27138e1098">6</a></sup>, including the cultivated Upland cotton <i>Gossypium hirsutum</i> (AD)<sub>1</sub> and the extra-long staple (ELS) cotton <i>Gossypium barbadense</i> (AD)<sub>2</sub>. However, our understanding of the molecular mechanism that facilitates the success of allopolyploids and the formation of agronomic traits remains limited.</p><p>Cotton provides the most important raw material for the textile industry and consequently profoundly affects the world economy and daily human life. The cotton genus <i>Gossypium</i> contains 45 diploid (2n = 26) and six tetraploid (2n = 52) species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Hawkins, J. S., Kim, H., Nason, J. D., Wing, R. A. &amp; Wendel, J. F. Differential lineage-specific amplification of transposable elements is responsible for genome size variation in Gossypium. Genome Res. 16, 1252–1261 (2006)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d27138e1119">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Grover, C. et al. Molecular confirmation of species status for the allopolyploid cotton species, Gossypium ekmanianum Wittmack. Genet Resour Crop Ev. 62, 103–114 (2015)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d27138e1122">8</a></sup>, among which only four species, including two tetraploids (<i>G. hirsutum</i> and <i>G. barbadense</i>) and two diploids (<i>G. herbaceum</i> and <i>G. arboreum</i>), produce spinnable fiber. Diploid cottons are divided into eight cytogenetic genome groups, A-G and K. The sizes of genomes vary between groups due to the lineage-specific proliferation of retrotransposons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Hawkins, J. S., Kim, H., Nason, J. D., Wing, R. A. &amp; Wendel, J. F. Differential lineage-specific amplification of transposable elements is responsible for genome size variation in Gossypium. Genome Res. 16, 1252–1261 (2006)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d27138e1139">7</a></sup>. The D-group species have the smallest genome with <i>G. raimondii</i> (D<sub>5</sub>) of less than 880 Mb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Hendrix, B. &amp; Stewart, J. M. Estimation of the nuclear DNA content of gossypium species. Ann Bot. 95, 789–797 (2005)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d27138e1148">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. Repeated polyploidization of Gossypium genomes and the evolution of spinnable cotton fibres. Nature. 492, 423–427 (2012)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d27138e1151">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Wang, K. et al. The draft genome of a diploid cotton Gossypium raimondii. Nat Genet. 44, 1098–1103 (2012)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d27138e1154">11</a></sup>, whereas the genome of <i>G. arboreum</i> (A<sub>2</sub>) in the A-group is approximately 1,700 Mb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Li, F. et al. Genome sequence of the cultivated cotton Gossypium arboreum. Nat Genet. 46, 567–572 (2014)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d27138e1164">12</a></sup>. <i>G. hirsutum</i> and <i>G. barbadense</i> are considered classic natural allotetraploids that originated in the New World approximately 2 million years ago (Mya) from trans-oceanic hybridization between an A-genome ancestral African species, <i>G. herbaceum</i> (A<sub>1</sub>) or <i>G. arboreum</i> (A<sub>2</sub>) and a native D-genome species, <i>G. raimondii</i> or <i>G. gossypioides</i> (D<sub>6</sub>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Senchina, D. S. et al. Rate variation among nuclear genes and the age of polyploidy in Gossypium. Mol Biol Evol. 20, 633–643 (2003)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d27138e1193">13</a></sup>, followed by divergence from their common ancestor (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). These two allotetraploids are likely the oldest major allopolyploid crops<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. Repeated polyploidization of Gossypium genomes and the evolution of spinnable cotton fibres. Nature. 492, 423–427 (2012)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d27138e1201">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Marcussen, T. et al. Ancient hybridizations among the ancestral genomes of bread wheat. Science. 345, 1250092 (2014)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d27138e1204">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Chalhoub, B. et al. Plant genetics. Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome. Science. 345, 950–953 (2014)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d27138e1207">15</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Figure 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep14139/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep14139/MediaObjects/41598_2015_Article_BFsrep14139_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep14139/MediaObjects/41598_2015_Article_BFsrep14139_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="289" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>A schematic map of the evolution of allotetraploid cottons.</p><p>Allotetraploid cotton evolved from the natural hybridization between A- and D-genome species and has split into six species, including the widely cultivated <i>G. barbadense</i> (AD<sub>2</sub>) and <i>G. hirsutum</i> (AD<sub>1</sub>). Evolutionary time (in Mya) is indicated by a numbered axis; major evolutionary events are represented by arrows and concluded in boxes. A black star indicates a retrotransposon burst and a red star indicates a boom in pseudogene production. Gr, <i>G. raimondii</i>, a diploid species (D<sub>5</sub>); Gb, <i>G. barbadense</i>; Gh, <i>G. hirsutum</i>. Mature cotton fiber is shown for extra-long stable (ELS) cotton (<i>G. barbadense</i>, AD<sub>2</sub>) and Upland cotton (<i>G. hirsutum</i>, AD<sub>1</sub>).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep14139/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Cotton fiber is derived from single-celled, seed-borne hair (trichrome) and the development of fiber cells is largely synchronized in a cotton ball (fruit) in four overlapping stages: initiation, elongation, secondary cell wall synthesis and maturation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Kim, H. J. &amp; Triplett, B. A. Cotton fiber growth in planta and in vitro. Models for plant cell elongation and cell wall biogenesis. Plant Physiol. 127, 1361–1366 (2001)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d27138e1267">16</a></sup>. These processes provide an excellent model to dissect cell differentiation, elongation and cellulose biosynthesis. The rate and duration of the elongation stage determines fiber length and the secondary cell wall biosynthesis affects fiber strength and fineness<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Smart, L. B., Vojdani, F., Maeshima, M. &amp; Wilkins, T. A. Genes involved in osmoregulation during turgor-driven cell expansion of developing cotton fibers are differentially regulated. Plant Physiol. 116, 1539–1549 (1998)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d27138e1271">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Ruan, Y. L., Llewellyn, D. J. &amp; Furbank, R. T. The control of single-celled cotton fiber elongation by developmentally reversible gating of plasmodesmata and coordinated expression of sucrose and K+ transporters and expansin. Plant Cell. 13, 47–60 (2001)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d27138e1274">18</a></sup>. The Upland cotton <i>G. hirsutum</i> constitutes ~90% of the annual cotton output and is characterized by its high yield yet moderate fiber qualities, whereas the ELS cotton <i>G. barbadense</i> produces over 5% of the world’s cotton and is famous for its superior quality fiber, as based on the length, strength and fineness of its fibers (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). Therefore, <i>G. barbadense</i> is preferred for the production of high-grade or special cotton textiles.</p><p>Although <i>G. barbadense</i> and <i>G. hirsutum</i> may share a common progenitor, the two species substantially differ, which has hindered the transfer of the superior fiber traits of <i>G. barbadense</i> to <i>G. hirsutum via</i> inter-species hybridization. This transfer has been particularly hindered by distorted segregation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Wang, P. et al. Inheritance of long staple fiber quality traits of Gossypium barbadense in G. hirsutum background using CSILs. Theor Appl Genet. 124, 1415–1428 (2012)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d27138e1306">19</a></sup>. The recently released genome sequences of <i>G. hirsutum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Li, F. et al. Genome sequence of cultivated Upland cotton (Gossypium hirsutum TM-1) provides insights into genome evolution. Nat Biotechnol. 33, 524–530 (2015)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d27138e1313">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Zhang, T. et al. Sequencing of allotetraploid cotton (Gossypium hirsutum L. acc. TM-1) provides a resource for fiber improvement. Nat Biotechnol. 33, 531–537 (2015)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d27138e1316">21</a></sup> and the two extant diploid progenitor species, <i>G. raimondii</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. Repeated polyploidization of Gossypium genomes and the evolution of spinnable cotton fibres. Nature. 492, 423–427 (2012)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d27138e1322">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Wang, K. et al. The draft genome of a diploid cotton Gossypium raimondii. Nat Genet. 44, 1098–1103 (2012)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d27138e1325">11</a></sup> and <i>G. arboreum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Li, F. et al. Genome sequence of the cultivated cotton Gossypium arboreum. Nat Genet. 46, 567–572 (2014)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d27138e1331">12</a></sup>, have provided insight into cotton evolution and a wealth of resources for fiber improvement. A genome sequence of <i>G. barbadense</i> will further our understanding of the dynamics of genome structures and the genetic driving force associated with allotetraploids, particularly the molecular basis of the formation of fibers with superior traits.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequence and assembly</h3><p>We adopted a progressive strategy to sequence the allotetraploid genome of <i>G. barbadense</i> cv. Xinhai21 (AD)<sub>2</sub>. First, the genomes of the extant diploid species of <i>G. arboreum</i> (A<sub>2</sub>) and <i>G. raimondii</i> (D<sub>5</sub>) were separately sequenced and assembled. These sequences, together with their published genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. Repeated polyploidization of Gossypium genomes and the evolution of spinnable cotton fibres. Nature. 492, 423–427 (2012)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d27138e1366">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Li, F. et al. Genome sequence of the cultivated cotton Gossypium arboreum. Nat Genet. 46, 567–572 (2014)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d27138e1369">12</a></sup>, were used as references for early assortments of the primary reads into A<sub>t</sub> and D<sub>t</sub> subgenomes. Then the sequences were assembled into A<sub>t</sub> and D<sub>t</sub> contigs and scaffolds (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 1</a>). A total of 471 Gb (188× genome equivalent) of data were separately produced using the Roche 454, Illumina Hiseq2000 and PacBio SMRT sequencing platforms (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 2</a>). The particularly long reads (22.67 Gb) obtained from PacBio SMRT and the assembled 53-Gb contigs of the BAC pool further reduced the effects of repeats in the assembly, yielding a gap reduction of 63.4% (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>). Finally, we used the ultra-dense linkage map consisting of 4,999,048 single-nucleotide polymorphism (SNP) loci<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Wang, S. et al. Sequence-based ultra-dense genetic and physical maps reveal structural variations of allopolyploid cotton genomes. Genome Biol. 16, 108 (2015)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d27138e1391">22</a></sup> to assign and orient the 26 chromosomes and validate the polyploidy genome of <i>G. barbadense</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>). We detected only 20 Mb sequences in which the subgenome classification of homoeologous sequences conflicted between the sequence assembly and the linkage mapping strategies, which was likely due to sequence conversions between the two subgenomes. A total of 208 Mb sequences with erroneous inter-chromosomal joins in the A<sub>t</sub> or D<sub>t</sub> subgenome were detected and then corrected.</p><p>The combination of these methods resulted in a draft genome for <i>G. barbadense</i> with an overall contig N50 of 72 kilobases (kb) and scaffold N50 of 503 kb covering 1.395 Gigabases (Gb) of the A subgenome (A<sub>t</sub>) and 0.776 Gb of the D subgenome (D<sub>t</sub>) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#Tab1">Table 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>). In total, ~88% of the 2.470 Gb genome was based on k-mer estimation (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>). The genome contains at least 63.2% repeated sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 3</a>), half of which are transposable elements (TEs) that primarily consist of long-terminal-repeat retrotransposons (LTR retrons) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics of <i>G. barbadense</i> genome features.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/srep14139/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Figure 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep14139/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep14139/MediaObjects/41598_2015_Article_BFsrep14139_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep14139/MediaObjects/41598_2015_Article_BFsrep14139_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="467" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><i>G. barbadense</i> genome atlas and chromosome-level translocations.</p><p>(<b>a</b>) Genome atlas. The outermost circle represents the numbered chromosomes of A<sub>t</sub> and D<sub>t</sub> and chromosome sizes are marked by a scale plate. The three tracks moving inside successively represent gene, peudogene and repeat densities (calculated with 1 Mb windows) across the chromosomes. The core ribbon-link shows collinearity between A<sub>t</sub> and D<sub>t</sub>. (<b>b</b>,<b>c</b>) chromosomal translocations. The translocations among chromosome 2 and chromosome 3 of either A<sub>t</sub> or D<sub>t</sub> are indicated with blue lines (<b>b</b>) and those among chromosome 4 and chromosome 5 with blue and purple lines (<b>c</b>). The vertical colored lines from left to right represent chromosomes. The loci of <i>PRE1</i> implicated in fiber cell elongation are specifically marked with red in the chromosomes A05 and D04. Digits (01 to 13) after A, D or Gr indicate the chromosome of the A<sub>t</sub>/D<sub>t</sub> subgenome of <i>G. barbadense</i> or of <i>G. raimondii</i>, respectively.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep14139/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Gene annotation</h3><p>To initiate gene prediction, ~1 million expressed sequence tags (ESTs) that were generated using Roche 454 from a combination of 28 samples of eight tissues/organs collected at different development stages were mapped to the genome as gene models, which resulted in 40,502 and 37,024 protein-coding genes (CDSs) with an average length of 1,077 and 1,123 bp in the <i>G. barbadense</i> A<sub>t</sub> and D<sub>t</sub> subgenomes, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#Tab1">Table 1</a>) and falling in the same range as the number and length of CDSs of <i>G. raimondii</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. Repeated polyploidization of Gossypium genomes and the evolution of spinnable cotton fibres. Nature. 492, 423–427 (2012)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d27138e1842">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Wang, K. et al. The draft genome of a diploid cotton Gossypium raimondii. Nat Genet. 44, 1098–1103 (2012)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d27138e1845">11</a></sup>. Further evaluation using the 70-Gb RNA-Seq data <i>via</i> Illumina supported 96.6% of the predicted CDSs. The 77,526 predicted genes were annotated, which revealed 62,966 functional genes, excluding 8,518 A<sub>t</sub> and 6,042 D<sub>t</sub> genes (~20%) that lacked clear biological functions.</p><p>To examine the influence of allopolyploidy on gene contents, we classified cotton genes into domain families. The composition and family size of the assigned Pfam domain families are overall identical in <i>G. barbadense</i> A<sub>t</sub> and D<sub>t</sub>, <i>G. raimondii</i> and, to a lesser extent, <i>G. arboreum</i>. Protein domains whose function was clearly annotated, such as protein kinase, cytochrome P450 and pentatrico-peptide repeat (PPR), were commonly over-represented as large families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>) as in other angiosperm plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Arabidopsis Genome I. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature. 408, 796–815 (2000)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d27138e1880">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing P. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature. 436, 793–800 (2005)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d27138e1883">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature. 463, 178–183 (2010)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d27138e1886">25</a></sup>. Although most domains (3,039 out of 3,674) were maintained in each subgenome after the two were merged, pronounced changes in family size occurred, as exemplified by more ring finger domain (PF13639) and leucine rich repeat (PF13855) genes in the diploid D genome than in either A<sub>t</sub> or D<sub>t</sub> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 4</a>). This finding suggested that super-large families have evolved faster than others and tended to lose members in polyploids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Brenchley, R. et al. Analysis of the bread wheat genome using whole-genome shotgun sequencing. Nature. 491, 705–710 (2012)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d27138e1898">26</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Genome evolution</h3><p>A total of 21,639 pairs of orthologs were identified between A<sub>t</sub> and D<sub>t</sub>. We compared the <i>Ks</i> values of orthologous gene pairs among <i>G. barbadense</i> (Gb), <i>G. hirsutum</i> (Gh) and <i>G. raimondii</i> (Gr) at the whole-genome level (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 5</a>). A peak of 0.011 in both GbD<sub>t</sub>:GrD<sub>5</sub> and GhD<sub>t</sub>:GrD<sub>5</sub> indicates that the D<sub>t</sub> subgenome in of both allotetraploids originated from a <i>G. raimondii</i>-like progenitor<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Page, J. T. et al. Insights into the evolution of cotton diploids and polyploids from whole-genome re-sequencing. G3. 3, 1809–1818 (2013)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d27138e1947">27</a></sup>. The peak values for GbA<sub>t</sub>:GaA<sub>2</sub> and GhA<sub>t</sub>:GaA<sub>2</sub> are lower but again similar, presumably due to a shorter time since divergence compared to that between D-genome species. In addition, unlike <i>G. raimondii</i>, which is a wild species, <i>G. arboreum</i> has long been cultivated in African and Asian countries. Another pair of similar <i>Ks</i> peaks (0.005) of GbA<sub>t</sub>:GhA<sub>t</sub> and GbD<sub>t</sub>:GhD<sub>t</sub> further supports the common origin of the two allotetraploid cottons and suggests their later divergence approximately 1 Mya (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>). Based on the larger <i>Ks</i> value (0.04) for A<sub>t</sub>:D<sub>t</sub>, we estimated the divergence time between the <i>Gossypium</i> A- and D-genome species to be approximately 8 Mya, consistent with previous estimates that were based on a few single-copy genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Senchina, D. S. et al. Rate variation among nuclear genes and the age of polyploidy in Gossypium. Mol Biol Evol. 20, 633–643 (2003)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d27138e1992">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Page, J. T. et al. Insights into the evolution of cotton diploids and polyploids from whole-genome re-sequencing. G3. 3, 1809–1818 (2013)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d27138e1995">27</a></sup>. The <i>Ks</i> values of paralogs in the two subgenomes of <i>G. barbadense</i> both peak at 0.4–0.5, which indicate ancient WGD event(s) that occurred 50–70 Mya (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>), which were responsible for the repeated genome expansion in <i>Gossypium</i> after divergence from the <i>Theobroma cacao</i> lineage more than 60 Mya<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. Repeated polyploidization of Gossypium genomes and the evolution of spinnable cotton fibres. Nature. 492, 423–427 (2012)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d27138e2015">10</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Figure 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep14139/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep14139/MediaObjects/41598_2015_Article_BFsrep14139_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep14139/MediaObjects/41598_2015_Article_BFsrep14139_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="494" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Evolutionary analysis of the <i>G. barbadense</i> genome.</p><p>(<b>a</b>) <i>Ks</i> distribution of orthologs in cotton genomes. Data are grouped into 0.001 Ks units. (<b>b</b>) <i>Ks</i> distribution of paralogs in the <i>G. barbadense</i> genome. Data are grouped into 0.01 Ks units and the peak region corresponds to 50–70 million years. (<b>c</b>) The distribution curve of the insertion times in the LTR retrons in the <i>G. barbadense</i> genome. The LTR retrons bursts are separated by dashed lines. (<b>d</b>) <i>Ks</i> distribution of pseudogenes with their closest functional paralogous genes. Data are grouped into 0.001-Ks units. The genomes of allotetraploid cottons are labeled using A<sub>t</sub>/D<sub>t</sub> and the genomes of <i>G. arboreum</i> (A<sub>2</sub>) and <i>G. raimondii</i> (D<sub>5</sub>) are labeled using Ga and Gr.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep14139/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Both the A<sub>t</sub> and D<sub>t</sub> subgenomes of <i>G. barbadense</i> demonstrate a high level of co-linearity with the <i>G. raimondii</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. Repeated polyploidization of Gossypium genomes and the evolution of spinnable cotton fibres. Nature. 492, 423–427 (2012)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d27138e2099">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Wang, K. et al. The draft genome of a diploid cotton Gossypium raimondii. Nat Genet. 44, 1098–1103 (2012)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d27138e2102">11</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>). A total of 21 Megabase (Mb) sequences in the D<sub>t</sub> and 7.4 Mb in the A<sub>t</sub> were identified as inter-subgenome translocation regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 7</a>). Two of three major intra-subgenomic rearrangements between chrA2/chrA3 and chrA4/chrA5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Brubaker, C. L., Paterson, A. H. &amp; Wendel, J. F. Comparative genetic mapping of allotetraploid cotton and its diploid progenitors. Genome. 42, 184–203 (1999)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d27138e2117">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Yu, J. Z. et al. A high-density simple sequence repeat and single nucleotide polymorphism genetic map of the tetraploid cotton genome. G3. 2, 43–58 (2012)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d27138e2120">29</a></sup> were observed in the A<sub>t</sub> of both of the allotetraploid cottons but absent in the D<sub>t</sub> or <i>G. raimondii</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>), suggesting that the two translocations likely occurred after the separation of the A and D genomes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Genomic plasticity and evolution</h3><p>We identified 6,014/2,422 complete LTR retrons with an average length of 9,256/8,130 bp in A<sub>t</sub>/D<sub>t</sub> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">Supplementary Tables 6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">7</a>), similar to the numbers of LTR retrons in <i>G. hirsutum</i> A<sub>t</sub> and D<sub>t</sub>, <i>G. arboreum</i> and <i>G. raimondii</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 8</a>). The singleton LTR retrons ratio is 83.5% in A<sub>t</sub> and 82.2% in D<sub>t</sub> (compared with 85.4% in <i>G. raimondii</i> and 73.2% in <i>G. arboreum</i>), close to that (86%) in the genome of a gymnosperm tree, <i>Picea abies</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Nystedt, B. et al. The Norway spruce genome sequence and conifer genome evolution. Nature. 497, 579–584 (2013)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d27138e2183">30</a></sup> (an indication of high divergence).</p><p>The TE proliferations in <i>G. barbadense</i> and <i>G. hirsutum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Li, F. et al. Genome sequence of cultivated Upland cotton (Gossypium hirsutum TM-1) provides insights into genome evolution. Nat Biotechnol. 33, 524–530 (2015)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d27138e2195">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Zhang, T. et al. Sequencing of allotetraploid cotton (Gossypium hirsutum L. acc. TM-1) provides a resource for fiber improvement. Nat Biotechnol. 33, 531–537 (2015)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d27138e2198">21</a></sup>, represented by insertions of LTR retrons based on estimations according to the sequence divergence between the left and right soloLTR<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="SanMiguel, P. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Evidence that a Recent Increase in Maize Genome Size was Caused by the Massive Amplification of Intergene Retrotransposons. Annals of Botany. 82 (suppl 1), 37–44 (1998)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d27138e2202">31</a></sup>, have increased since 20 Mya and three distinct bursts were identified. Interestingly, the first two bursts appear to successively pre-date the divergence and the re-unification of the diploid A/D genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a>). The LTR retrons clearly show type-specific and subgenome-biased proliferations (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a>). Their insertion rates in the A genome appear consistently higher than those in the D genome. For example, a large number (9.15%) of LTR retrons burst at 5 Mya and decreased thereafter in A<sub>t</sub>, whereas a substantially lower and flat peak appeared 3–5 Mya in D<sub>t</sub> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a>). This peak at least partly accounts for the 1.7-fold more LTR retrons in the former genome. However, the faster loss of LTR retrons in the D genome may also be responsible for genome size variations and the different rates of genome expansion<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Hawkins, J. S., Proulx, S. R., Rapp, R. A. &amp; Wendel, J. F. Rapid D. N. A. loss as a counterbalance to genome expansion through retrotransposon proliferation in plants. P Natl Acad Sci USA. 106, 17811–17816 (2009)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d27138e2220">32</a></sup>. Notably, the third asymmetric activities of transposons differ between <i>G. barbadense</i> and <i>G. hirsutum</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a>), which suggests a possible cause of subgenome divergence that may have promoted the speciation of allotetraploid cottons beginning approximately 1 Mya (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). These observations indicate that the genome-specific differential dynamics of TE proliferations could be a major force that has driven the rapid evolution and diversification of <i>Gossypium</i> species, which may also be inferred in other flowering plants.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Pseudogenization prior to and after polyploidization</h3><p>Pseudogenes are disabled copies resembling functional genes that have been retained in the genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Brenchley, R. et al. Analysis of the bread wheat genome using whole-genome shotgun sequencing. Nature. 491, 705–710 (2012)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d27138e2249">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Zou, C., Lehti-Shiu, M. D., Thibaud-Nissen, F., Prakash, T., Buell, C. R. &amp; Shiu, S. H. Evolutionary and expression signatures of pseudogenes in Arabidopsis and rice. Plant Physiol. 151, 3–15 (2009)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d27138e2252">33</a></sup>. They can be grouped into three categories: duplicated (derived from gene duplication), processed (generated by the integration of reversed-transcribed cDNAs into genomes) and fragmented (neither processed nor duplicated)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Zou, C., Lehti-Shiu, M. D., Thibaud-Nissen, F., Prakash, T., Buell, C. R. &amp; Shiu, S. H. Evolutionary and expression signatures of pseudogenes in Arabidopsis and rice. Plant Physiol. 151, 3–15 (2009)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d27138e2256">33</a></sup>. To further investigate the influence of TE bursts and polyploidization on the cotton genomic architecture, we predicted pseudogenes in <i>G. barbadense</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 9</a>) and classified them into the three categories (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 8</a>), most of which are silenced without any detectable transcripts in all tissues examined.</p><p>Each subgenome of <i>G. barbadense</i> contains more predicted pseudogenes than the diploid genome of <i>G. raimondii</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 9</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 8</a>), implying an accelerated pseudogenization after allopolyploid formation. A substantial portion of the pseudogenes in A<sub>t</sub> and D<sub>t</sub> showed a high sequence identity (above 90%, for example) with their parental genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 9</a>), suggesting an insufficient duration for degeneration in recently formed pseudogenes. As expected, the <i>Ka/Ks</i> distributions indicate a substantially weaker natural selection on pseudogenes than on protein-coding genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>), which is likely due to a loss of function in pseudogenes. The <i>Ks</i> value peaks at 0.06–0.1 corresponding to 11–20 Mya (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#Fig3">Fig. 3d</a>) and this boom of pseudogenization correlates with an LTR retron burst prior to the divergence of the A and D genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a>). The average expression levels of the genes with LTR retron insertion within a 20-kb region upstream of the start codon are generally lower (RPKM = 7.72) than those of genes lacking this insertion (RPKM = 13) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 10</a>). Therefore, LTR retrons negatively affect the expression of nearby genes, which may promote pseudogenization. These results suggest that cotton progenitors likely lost genes and experienced LTR retron bursts following the ancient WGD, which promoted diversification in <i>Gossypium</i> genomes; however, the role of TE-associated pseudogenization in the stabilization of subgenomes in polyploids requires a more detailed analysis.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Extra-long staple fiber formation</h3><p>We identified 2,483 and 1,879 genes that are specifically or preferentially expressed in fibers and the ovule, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">Supplementary Tables 11</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">12</a>). The highly active genes in the ovule are abundant in the protein families of nucleic acid binding/transcription factor activity and nutrient reservoir activity, whereas the up-regulated genes of fibers are enriched in several categories, such as those related to cytoskeleton, carbohydrate metabolism, cell wall biosynthesis and cellulose biosynthesis function (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">Supplementary Tables 13</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">14</a>).</p><p>Consistent with a previous report<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Yoo, M. J. &amp; Wendel, J. F. Comparative evolutionary and developmental dynamics of the cotton (Gossypium hirsutum) fiber transcriptome. PLoS Genet. 10, e1004073 (2014)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d27138e2337">34</a></sup>, equal numbers of genes in the A<sub>t</sub> and D<sub>t</sub> subgenomes demonstrated biased expression patterns (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">Supplementary Tables 15</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">16</a>). Transcription factors play an important role in controlling agronomic novelty and the MYB and homeodomain-containing factors have been shown to be key regulators of cotton fiber traits development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. Repeated polyploidization of Gossypium genomes and the evolution of spinnable cotton fibres. Nature. 492, 423–427 (2012)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d27138e2352">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Walford, S. A., Wu, Y., Llewellyn, D. J. &amp; Dennis, E. S. GhMYB25-like: a key factor in early cotton fibre development. Plant J. 65, 785–797 (2011)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d27138e2355">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Zhang, M. et al. Spatiotemporal manipulation of auxin biosynthesis in cotton ovule epidermal cells enhances fiber yield and quality. Nat Biotechnol. 29, 453–458 (2011)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d27138e2358">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Shan, C. M. et al. Control of cotton fibre elongation by a homeodomain transcription factor GhHOX3. Nat Commun. 5, 5519 (2014)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d27138e2361">37</a></sup>. We then analyzed transcription factor genes expressed in <i>G. barbadense</i> fiber in detail (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 17</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 11</a>). <b><i>P</i></b>aclobutrazol <b><i>Re</i></b>sistance (<i>PRE</i>) genes encode a group of transcription regulators known in other plants to promote cell elongation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Chen, Z. J. Genetic and epigenetic mechanisms for gene expression and phenotypic variation in plant polyploids. Annu Rev Plant Biol. 58, 377–406 (2007)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d27138e2384">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Bai, M. Y., Fan, M., Oh, E. &amp; Wang, Z. Y. A triple helix-loop-helix/basic helix-loop-helix cascade controls cell elongation downstream of multiple hormonal and environmental signaling pathways in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell. 24, 4917–4929 (2012)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d27138e2387">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Zhang, L. Y. et al. Antagonistic HLH/bHLH transcription factors mediate brassinosteroid regulation of cell elongation and plant development in rice and Arabidopsis. Plant Cell. 21, 3767–3780 (2009)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d27138e2390">40</a></sup>. We identified 13 <i>PRE</i> family genes in <i>G. raimondii</i>; their 26 orthologous genes were recovered in <i>G. barbadense</i>. Analyzing the <i>PRE</i>-containing synteny blocks in plants revealed that cacao<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Argout, X. et al. The genome of Theobroma cacao. Nat Genet. 43, 101–108 (2011)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d27138e2407">41</a></sup> has five <i>PRE</i> genes, each of which has at least two orthologs in the <i>Gossypium</i> diploid genomes or the allotetraploid subgenomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 12</a>). This expansion of <i>PRE</i> genes in cotton may have occurred during a complex 5–6-fold polyploidy process<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. Repeated polyploidization of Gossypium genomes and the evolution of spinnable cotton fibres. Nature. 492, 423–427 (2012)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d27138e2427">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Wang, K. et al. The draft genome of a diploid cotton Gossypium raimondii. Nat Genet. 44, 1098–1103 (2012)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d27138e2430">11</a></sup>, which was followed by differential gene loss but the retention of the ancient orthologs. Interestingly, two <i>PRE</i> genes are located in the two A<sub>t</sub> translocation regions (chrA2/chrA3 and chrA4/chrA5) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 12</a>). In cotton, <i>PRE</i> genes are preferentially expressed in young tissues (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#Fig4">Fig. 4b,c</a>), which is consistent with their role in controlling cell size. Moreover, the expression of A<sub>t</sub> and D<sub>t</sub> <i>PRE</i> homoeologous genes was biased in <i>G. barbadense</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">Supplementary Tables 11</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">12</a>). In particular, the expression level of A<sub>t</sub>-subgenome <i>PRE1</i> was high and fiber specific, whereas the expression the D<sub>t</sub> homoeolog was nearly undetectable (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a>). The A<sub>t</sub>-specific expression of a cell growth regulator provides a clue to support the origin or early evolution of spinnable fiber in the A-genome species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. Repeated polyploidization of Gossypium genomes and the evolution of spinnable cotton fibres. Nature. 492, 423–427 (2012)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d27138e2482">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Wang, K. et al. The draft genome of a diploid cotton Gossypium raimondii. Nat Genet. 44, 1098–1103 (2012)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d27138e2485">11</a></sup>. The expansion and subsequent selection<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Wang, K. et al. The draft genome of a diploid cotton Gossypium raimondii. Nat Genet. 44, 1098–1103 (2012)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d27138e2489">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Yoo, M. J. &amp; Wendel, J. F. Comparative evolutionary and developmental dynamics of the cotton (Gossypium hirsutum) fiber transcriptome. PLoS Genet. 10, e1004073 (2014)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d27138e2492">34</a></sup> of <i>PRE</i> genes in <i>Gossypium</i> may have increased their regulatory activity and recruited specific member(s) for the rapid and extensive elongation of cotton fiber (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#Fig1">Figs 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#Fig4">4c</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Figure 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep14139/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep14139/MediaObjects/41598_2015_Article_BFsrep14139_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep14139/MediaObjects/41598_2015_Article_BFsrep14139_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="555" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Expansion and diversification of <i>PRE</i> genes in <i>Gossypium</i>.</p><p>(<b>a</b>) Phylogenetic analysis of <i>PRE</i> family genes in <i>Amborella trichopoda</i>, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>G. raimondii</i> and <i>G. barbadense</i>. Subfamilies are overlaid with different colors and the curved dotted lines indicate homoeologous gene pairs expressed in fiber. (<b>b</b>) <i>GbPRE1</i> (GOBAR_AA33780, GOBAR_DD03693) is a fiber-specific gene with strong A<sub>t</sub> bias expression. The expression levels (RPKM) in ovules (0 DPA) and fiber cells (5, 10, 20 and 30 DPA) are shown. Detailed expression data are provided in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 10</a>. (<b>c</b>) Hierarchical clustering analysis of expression of <i>PRE</i> genes in <i>G. barbadense</i>. LB, leaf bud; YL, young leaf; ML, mature leaf; O, ovule; F, fiber; DPA, days post-anthesis.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep14139/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Cellulose, which consists of linear chains of β (1–4)-linked D-glucose, is the major component of higher plant cell walls and the most abundant biopolymer on land. Plants express multiple cellulose synthases (CesAs) that, together with CesA-associated proteins, form the cellulose synthase complex<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Gu, Y. et al. Identification of a cellulose synthase-associated protein required for cellulose biosynthesis. P Natl Acad Sci USA. 107, 12866–12871 (2010)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d27138e2579">42</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="McFarlane, H. E., Doring, A. &amp; Persson, S. The cell biology of cellulose synthesis. Annu Rev Plant Biol. 65, 69–94 (2014)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d27138e2582">43</a></sup>. Cotton fiber is distinct not only in its extensive elongation (ELS cotton fiber is longer than 35 mm) but also in its exceptionally high amount of cellulose, which constitutes more than 95% of the dry weight of mature fiber<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Kim, H. J. &amp; Triplett, B. A. Cotton fiber growth in planta and in vitro. Models for plant cell elongation and cell wall biogenesis. Plant Physiol. 127, 1361–1366 (2001)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d27138e2586">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Gou, J. Y., Wang, L. J., Chen, S. P., Hu, W. L. &amp; Chen, X. Y. Gene expression and metabolite profiles of cotton fiber during cell elongation and secondary cell wall synthesis. Cell Res. 17, 422–434 (2007)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d27138e2589">44</a></sup>. Notably, the first higher plant cellulose synthase gene was cloned from cotton<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Pear, J. R., Kawagoe, Y., Schreckengost, W. E. &amp; Delmer, D. P., Stalker DM. Higher plants contain homologs of the bacterial celA genes encoding the catalytic subunit of cellulose synthase. P Natl Acad Sci USA. 93, 12637–12642 (1996)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d27138e2593">45</a></sup>. Ten, 14 and 15 CesA genes are expressed in <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Gu, Y. et al. Identification of a cellulose synthase-associated protein required for cellulose biosynthesis. P Natl Acad Sci USA. 107, 12866–12871 (2010)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d27138e2599">42</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="McFarlane, H. E., Doring, A. &amp; Persson, S. The cell biology of cellulose synthesis. Annu Rev Plant Biol. 65, 69–94 (2014)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d27138e2602">43</a></sup>, <i>G. arboreum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Li, F. et al. Genome sequence of the cultivated cotton Gossypium arboreum. Nat Genet. 46, 567–572 (2014)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d27138e2609">12</a></sup> and <i>G. raimondii</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. Repeated polyploidization of Gossypium genomes and the evolution of spinnable cotton fibres. Nature. 492, 423–427 (2012)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d27138e2615">10</a></sup>, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#Fig5">Fig. 5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#Tab2">Table 2</a>). We identified 29 CesA genes, including 14 A<sub>t</sub> and 15 D<sub>t</sub>, in the <i>G. barbadense</i> genome, whereas 30 (14 A<sub>t</sub> and 16 D<sub>t</sub>) CesA genes were identified in <i>G. hirsutum</i>; most CesA genes had been retained after the merger of the A and D genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#Tab2">Table 2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 13</a>). Compared to <i>Arabidopsis</i>, each cotton genome or subgenome contains more genes in the CesA3, CesA4, CesA7 and CesA8 clades. Notably, chromosome 5 of both the A<sub>t</sub> and D<sub>t</sub> subgenomes of <i>G. barbadense</i> (GOBAR_AA25282, GOBAR_AA25287/GOBAR_DD32643, GOBAR_DD32648 and GOBAR_DD32650) and <i>G. hirsutum</i> (Gh_A05G3959, Gh_A05G3965, Gh_A05G3967/Gh_D05G0077, Gh_D05G0079 and Gh_D05G0084) as well as <i>G. arboreum</i> and <i>G. raimondii</i> contain a CesA cluster composed of 3 or, rarely, 2 genes, in addition to the CesA-like (CSL) genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#Tab2">Table 2</a>); thus, the duplication(s) occurred in the ancient cotton genome. </p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Cellulose synthase (CesA) genes in four cotton species.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/srep14139/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Figure 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep14139/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep14139/MediaObjects/41598_2015_Article_BFsrep14139_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep14139/MediaObjects/41598_2015_Article_BFsrep14139_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="421" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Cotton CesA genes and their expression in developing fiber cells of <i>G. barbadense</i> and <i>G. hirsutum.</i></p><p>(<b>a</b>) CesA genes from four cotton species were clustered (left) <i>via</i> MAGE5 using the maximum likelihood method. <i>G. arboreum</i> (Cotton_A) and <i>G. raimondii</i> (Gorai) contain 14 and 15 CesA genes, respectively, which are shown in the left column. The heat map (middle) shows the transcript level (FPKM, Reads Per Kilobase of exon model per Million mapped reads) of each homeologous gene in <i>G. barbadense</i> and <i>G. hirsutum</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#Tab2">Table 2</a>) fibers at different DPA. The relative expression level in the two allotetraploid cottons was compared (right). CesA1, CesA3 and CesA6 are implicated in primary cell wall biosynthesis and CesA4, CesA7 and CesA8 are implicated in secondary cell wall biosynthesis. (<b>b</b>) Temporal expression patterns of secondary cell wall <i>CesA</i> genes (CesA7, CesA4 and CesA8 clades) in <i>G. barbadense</i> and <i>G. hirsutum</i> fiber. Note that the expression was generally delayed in <i>G. barbadense</i> fiber. X-axis: day post-anthesis. Y-axis: FPKM.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep14139/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Although not exclusively, plant CesAs have functionally diverged into two major classes responsible for either primary cell wall or secondary cell wall biosynthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Gu, Y. et al. Identification of a cellulose synthase-associated protein required for cellulose biosynthesis. P Natl Acad Sci USA. 107, 12866–12871 (2010)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d27138e3457">42</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="McFarlane, H. E., Doring, A. &amp; Persson, S. The cell biology of cellulose synthesis. Annu Rev Plant Biol. 65, 69–94 (2014)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d27138e3460">43</a></sup>. Whereas spinnable cotton fiber evolved in the A-genome species and further developed in AD allotetraploids, the CesA gene family has not undergone expansion in any of the three cultivated cotton species sequenced. However, cotton fiber expresses many (at least 25) CesA genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#Fig5">Fig. 5</a>), demonstrating an enrichment of cellulose synthases in fiber cells. A comparison of the two allotetraploid cottons revealed that the secondary cell wall genes CesA4, CesA7 and CesA8 showed a delayed (&gt;5 days) and more drastic up-regulation in <i>G. barbadense</i> fiber than in <i>G. hirsutum</i> fiber (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#Fig5">Fig. 5</a>), which indicates a prolonged duration of fiber elongation and a high activity of cellulose biosynthesis in the secondary cell wall formation stage. Additionally, this temporal expression pattern suggests that the functional allocation of CesA members to primary and secondary wall biosynthesis, which is primarily based on <i>Arabidopsis</i> research<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Gu, Y. et al. Identification of a cellulose synthase-associated protein required for cellulose biosynthesis. P Natl Acad Sci USA. 107, 12866–12871 (2010)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d27138e3480">42</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="McFarlane, H. E., Doring, A. &amp; Persson, S. The cell biology of cellulose synthesis. Annu Rev Plant Biol. 65, 69–94 (2014)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d27138e3483">43</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Taylor-Teeples, M. et al. An Arabidopsis gene regulatory network for secondary cell wall synthesis. Nature. 517, 571–575 (2015)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d27138e3486">46</a></sup>, are likely conserved in angiosperms. Thus, both the retention of CesA family members and the expression pattern of functionally specialized genes in <i>G. barbadense</i> support the formation of extra-long and high-grade cotton fiber.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Terpene synthases and the evolution of cotton phytoalexins</h3><p>Terpenoids constitute a large family of natural compounds and play diverse roles in plant-environment interactions. Cotton plants accumulate a specialized group of cadinene-type sesquiterpenoids (including gossypol) that function as phytoalexins against pathogens and pests<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Mao, Y. B. et al. Silencing a cotton bollworm P450 monooxygenase gene by plant-mediated RNAi impairs larval tolerance of gossypol. Nat Biotechnol. 25, 1307–1313 (2007)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d27138e3501">47</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Tao, X.
