Y., Xue, X. Y., Huang, Y. P., Chen, X. Y. &amp; Mao, Y. B. Gossypol-enhanced P450 gene pool contributes to cotton bollworm tolerance to a pyrethroid insecticide. Mol Ecol. 21, 4371–4385 (2012)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d27138e3504">48</a></sup>. However, these sesquiterpenoids also reduce the value of cotton seeds that are rich in oil and proteins. Terpene synthases (TPSs) are a family of enzymes responsible for the synthesis of various terpenes from the 10-, 15- and 20-carbon precursors assembled from the 5-carbon building blocks of IPP and its isomer DMAPP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Bohlmann, J., Meyer-Gauen, G. &amp; Croteau, R. Plant terpenoid synthases: molecular biology and phylogenetic analysis. P Natl Acad Sci USA. 95, 4126–4133 (1998)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d27138e3508">49</a></sup>. A manual search of the <i>G. barbadense</i> genome with TPS N- and C-terminal domains (PF01397 and PF03936) identified 115 TPS genes, including 44 monoterpene, 59 sesquiterpene and 8 diterpene synthases, as well as 4 triterpene (squalene) synthases. This number is higher than that in <i>T. cacao</i> (43), <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (34) and <i>Vitis vinifera</i> (98) and similar to that in <i>G. hirsutum</i> (110) but slightly less than twice that in <i>G. raimondii</i> (69).</p><p>The cotton sesquiterpene synthase (+)-δ-cadinene synthase (CDN) catalyzes the first step of gossypol biosynthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Chen, X. Y., Chen, Y., Heinstein, P. &amp; Davisson, V. J. Cloning, expression and characterization of (+)-delta-cadinene synthase: a catalyst for cotton phytoalexin biosynthesis. Arch Biochem Biophys. 324, 255–266 (1995)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d27138e3534">50</a></sup>. The <i>G. barbadense</i> genome harbors 19 <i>CDN</i> family genes (sharing &gt;80% nucleotide identity), whereas <i>G. raimondii</i>, <i>G. arboreum</i> and <i>G. hirsutum</i> harbor 11, 14 and 13 of these genes, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#Fig6">Fig. 6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 18</a>). These genes evolved faster than cotton speciation; thus, the <i>CDN</i> family evolved approximately 60 Mya based on the phylogenetics of cotton plants (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). The <i>CDN</i> subfamilie<i>s</i> A and E were found closer to the ancient type and duplicated after the divergence of the cotton and cacao lineages (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#Fig6">Fig. 6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 14</a>). The variable <i>CDN</i> gene numbers in cotton species possibly refer to recent small-scale duplication events, <i>e.g.</i>, <i>CDN-A</i> member duplication in the D genome ~1 Mya (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 18</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 14</a>). Thus, the <i>CDN</i> subfamilies in <i>Gossypium</i> represent an example of the rapid lineage-specific evolution of critical genes for specialized metabolites.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Figure 6"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 6</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep14139/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep14139/MediaObjects/41598_2015_Article_BFsrep14139_Fig6_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep14139/MediaObjects/41598_2015_Article_BFsrep14139_Fig6_HTML.jpg" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="793" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>Phylogenetic analysis of (+)-δ-cadinene synthase (CDN) family genes and their genome distribution.</p><p>(<b>a</b>) The amino acid sequences of CDNs of <i>G. arboreum</i> (Cotton_A), <i>G. raimondii</i> (Gorai), <i>G. hirsutum</i> (Gh) and <i>G. barbadense</i> (GOBAR) and <i>T. cacao</i> (Thecc) were used to build the phylogenetic tree using a neighbor-joining algorithm <i>via</i> the MEGA software. The <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> sesquiterpene synthase gene At5g23960 was used as a phylogenetic outgroup. (<b>b</b>) Chromosomal locations of the CDN genes in four <i>Gossypium</i> species as indicated.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep14139/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>ELS cotton likely produces one of the most resilient fibers in the plant kingdom; they are highly elongated and contain nearly pure cellulose. This draft sequence of the <i>G. barbadense</i> genome provides valuable genomic resources for studying various aspects of cotton. This draft sequence also facilitates breeding practices aimed at improving cotton fiber traits and increasing the production of high-quality biomass (cellulose).</p><p>The genomes of two or more parental species have combined to significantly change the genome structure and function of allopolyploid plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Chen, Z. J. Genetic and epigenetic mechanisms for gene expression and phenotypic variation in plant polyploids. Annu Rev Plant Biol. 58, 377–406 (2007)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d27138e3667">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Comai, L. et al. Phenotypic instability and rapid gene silencing in newly formed arabidopsis allotetraploids. Plant Cell. 12, 1551–1568 (2000)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d27138e3670">51</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Liu, B. &amp; Wendel, J. F. Non-Mendelian phenomenon in allopolyploid genome evolution. Curr Genomics. 3, 489–505 (2002)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d27138e3673">52</a></sup>. Inter-genomic chromosomal rearrangements, differential gene loss (the loss of some duplicates), gene conversion, divergence and the functional diversification of duplicated genes often arise with the onset of polyploidization<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Adams, K. L. &amp; Wendel, J. F. Novel patterns of gene expression in polyploid plants. Trends Genet. 21, 539–543 (2005)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d27138e3677">53</a></sup>. Our comparative analysis of cotton genomes also provides new insight into dynamic allopolyploidy processes, such as the mechanism <i>via</i> TE (LTR retrons) bursts and pseudogenization, which have significantly contributed to plant genome evolution and trait formation.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Plant materials</h3><p>Young leaves of <i>Gossypium barbadense</i> cv. Xinhai21, <i>G. arboreum</i> cv. Qingyangxiaozi and <i>G. raimondii</i> were collected from a single plant of each species for genomic DNA extraction and sequencing. For transcriptome sequencing, 28 samples from <i>G. barbadense</i> roots, stems, flowers, leaves, ovules and fibers were collected for total RNA extraction (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 19</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">DNA isolation, library construction and sequencing</h3><p>Genomic DNA was isolated from fresh cotton leaves using a previously described method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Lomsadze, A., Ter-Hovhannisyan, V., Chernoff, Y. O. &amp; Borodovsky, M. Gene identification in novel eukaryotic genomes by self-training algorithm. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, 6494–6506 (2005)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d27138e3719">54</a></sup>. The shotgun library (300–800 bp fragments) was prepared from 5 μg of DNA using a standard protocol and a total of 55,296,227 reads with an average length of 542 bp were produced <i>via</i> Roche 454 GS FLX to provide a 12-fold coverage of the genome. The paired-end libraries of different insertion sizes were constructed and 1,325,215,140 pairs of 100-bp reads were produced <i>via</i> Illumina Hiseq2000 (Illumina, San Diego, CA) to provide 105-fold coverage of the genome. The 3-, 5-, 8 and 20-kb mate-pair libraries were constructed by combining the GS FLX and Illumina mate-pair protocol and a total of 773,715,534 mate-pair reads were produced <i>via</i> Illumina Hiseq2000 to provide 61.9-fold sequencing coverage. The BAC library (insert, 80–120 kb) was constructed using the pCC1BAC vector (Epicentre Inc.) and Hind III enzyme digestion. The BAC clones were both-end sequenced using ABI 3730 and 20 BACs at a time were pooled and sequenced on Illumina Hiseq2000 to generate a 300-bp paired-end library.</p><p>For the PacBio library construction and sequencing, genomic DNA was sheared using a Covaris g-TUBE followed by purification <i>via</i> binding to pre-washed AMPure XP beads (Beckman Coulter Inc.). After end-repair, the blunt adapters were ligated, followed by exonuclease incubation to remove all un-ligated adapters and DNA. The final “SMRT bells” were annealed with primers and bound to the proprietary polymerase using the PacBio DNA/Polymerase Binding Kit P4 (Part Number 100–236–500) to form the “Binding Complex”. After dilution, the library was loaded onto the instrument with DNA Sequencing Kit 2.0 (Part Number 100–216–400) and a SMRT Cell 8Pac for sequencing. A primary filtering analysis was performed with the RS instrument and the secondary analysis was performed using the SMRT analysis pipeline version 2.1.0.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Genome assembly</h3><p>The genomes of two diploid cotton species, <i>G. arboreum</i> and <i>G. raimondii</i>, were each sequenced at 100-fold coverage using Illumina Hiseq2000. The assembly resulted in 3,767,593 contigs of 1.5 Gb for <i>G. arboreum</i> and 1,111,300 contigs of 788 Mb for <i>G. raimondii</i>. These contigs, together with the published genomic data of <i>G. raimondii</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. Repeated polyploidization of Gossypium genomes and the evolution of spinnable cotton fibres. Nature. 492, 423–427 (2012)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d27138e3760">10</a></sup> and <i>G. arboreum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Li, F. et al. Genome sequence of the cultivated cotton Gossypium arboreum. Nat Genet. 46, 567–572 (2014)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d27138e3767">12</a></sup>, were used as template for grouping of <i>G. barbadense</i> sequencing reads into subgenomes, which resulted in totally 44.9% of the reads being A<sub>t</sub>-unique, 26.9% being D<sub>t</sub>-unique and 9.7% being both sharing. The remaining 18.5% none hit reads were further grouped during subgenome during sequence assembly.</p><p>After subgenome grouping, the A<sub>t</sub> and D<sub>t</sub> subgenomes of <i>G. barbadense</i> were assembled separately using a combined strategy. The Roche 454 reads were first assembled using Newbler v2.3. In total, 773,548 contigs with an average length of 2.5 kb were produced. Illumina pair-end reads, mate-pair reads, PacBio SMRT reads and BAC ends were then successively mapped to the contigs to improve quality. The 59,868 contigs (BACtigs) with an N50 of 23.8 kb from 515 BAC pools were merged. These approaches resulted in 4,586 A<sub>t</sub> scaffolds and 2,186 D<sub>t</sub> scaffolds with a total size of 2.2 Gb and maximum length of 3.4 Mb. Data statistics are given in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/srep14139#Tab1">Table 1</a>.</p><p>Finally, a high-density genetic map of <i>G. hirsutum</i> cv. TM-1 × <i>G. barbadense</i> cv. Hai7124 containing 4,999,048 SNPs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Wang, S. et al. Sequence-based ultra-dense genetic and physical maps reveal structural variations of allopolyploid cotton genomes. Genome Biol. 16, 108 (2015)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d27138e3808">22</a></sup> was mapped to the <i>G. barbadense</i> assembly using the BWA program, which anchored 1.95 Gb or 88% of the assembled sequences and produced 26 pseudo-molecules (chromosomes).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Gene prediction and annotation</h3><p>Three gene prediction programs, GeneMark (v2.3a)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Lomsadze, A., Ter-Hovhannisyan, V., Chernoff, Y. O. &amp; Borodovsky, M. Gene identification in novel eukaryotic genomes by self-training algorithm. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, 6494–6506 (2005)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d27138e3823">54</a></sup>, Augustus (v2.5)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Stanke, M. &amp; Waack, S. Gene prediction with a hidden Markov model and a new intron submodel. Bioinformatics. 19 Suppl 2, ii215–225 (2003)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d27138e3827">55</a></sup> and FgeneSH<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Salamov, A. A. &amp; Solovyev, V. V. Ab initio gene finding in Drosophila genomic DNA. Genome Res. 10, 516–522 (2000)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d27138e3831">56</a></sup>, were used to predict protein-coding genes in the <i>G. barbadense</i> genome. A final gene model was produced by combining the three prediction results with an in-house developed program (GLAD), a tool that creates consensus gene lists by integrating evidence from homology, <i>de novo</i> prediction and RNA-Seq/EST data. Annotation was performed by comparing the predicted proteins with non-redundant proteins (nr) and the UniProt and KEGG databases. Blast2go<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Conesa, A., Gotz, S., Garcia-Gomez, J. M., Terol, J., Talon, M. &amp; Robles, M. Blast2GO: a universal tool for annotation, visualization and analysis in functional genomics research. Bioinformatics. 21, 3674–3676 (2005)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d27138e3842">57</a></sup> was used to assign preliminary GO terms to the predicted gene models. Transcription factors were predicted using PlantTFDB v3.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Jin, J., Zhang, H., Kong, L., Gao, G. &amp; Luo, J. PlantTFDB 3.0: a portal for the functional and evolutionary study of plant transcription factors. Nucleic Acids Res. 42, D1182–1187 (2014)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d27138e3846">58</a></sup>. Protein domain predictions were performed using RPS-BLAST with a coverage &gt;90%. The metabolic pathways were constructed using the KEGG database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Kanehisa, M., Goto, S., Kawashima, S., Okuno, Y. &amp; Hattori, M. The KEGG resource for deciphering the genome. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, D277–280 (2004)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d27138e3850">59</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Ortholog identification and <i>Ks</i> calculation</h3><p>Genes were classified into ortholog groups with OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J., Jr. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178–2189 (2003)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d27138e3867">60</a></sup> against OrthoMCL proteins (default parameters) [PMID: 12952885]. The orthologs between species, or homoeologs between the A<sub>t</sub> and D<sub>t</sub> subgenomes of <i>G. barbadense</i>, were defined using BLASTP based on the Bidirectional Best Hit (BBH) method with a sequence coverage &gt;30% and identity &gt;30%, followed by selection of the best match. The <i>Ka</i> and <i>Ks</i> between orthologs were calculated using the KaKs_Calculator<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Zhang, Z., Li, J., Zhao, X. Q., Wang, J., Wong, G. K. &amp; Yu, J. KaKs_Calculator: calculating Ka and Ks through model selection and model averaging. Genomics, proteomics &amp; bioinformatics. 4, 259–263 (2006)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d27138e3885">61</a></sup> <i>via</i> model averaging. The unique gene in each subgenome was defined using the following parameters: 1. protein sequence with no match according to BLASTP against proteins of the other subgenome with E-value 1E-3; and 2. the sum of the length of the high-scoring segment pairs (HSP) was less than 1/3 of the CDS length (<i>via</i> BLASTN) against the genome sequence of the other subgenome.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Repeat and LTR retrotransposon analysis</h3><p>Repetitive sequences were identified using RepeatScout with default parameters. The consensus sequences of each repeat family were used to identify repeat compositions in the genome <i>via</i> Censor. The complete LTR retron structures were predicted using LTR_finder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265–268 (2007)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d27138e3906">62</a></sup> and miniature inverted-repeat transposable elements (MITEs) were identified using MITE-Hunter<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Han, Y. &amp; Wessler, S. R. MITE-Hunter: a program for discovering miniature inverted-repeat transposable elements from genomic sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 38, e199 (2010)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d27138e3910">63</a></sup>. Individual LTR retrotransposons were clustered into the same family using the 80–80–80 rule: If two TIR sequences share 80% or higher similarity in at least 80% of their length with an alignment length longer than 80 bp, the two sequences were clustered into the same family<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Wicker, T. et al. A unified classification system for eukaryotic transposable elements. Nat Rev Genet. 8, 973–982 (2007)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d27138e3914">64</a></sup>.</p><p>The insertion ages of each full-length LTR retron were calculated based on the divergence between the left and right solo-LTR sequences using distmat from EMBOSS with the Kimura-2-parameter distance option and insertion ages were calculated according to the formula T = K/(2r) (K = Kimura distance value, average substitution rate r = 2.6 × 10<sup>–9</sup> in cotton).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Pseudogene identification</h3><p>Pseudogenes were predicted using Pseudopipe<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Zhang, Z., Carriero, N., Zheng, D., Karro, J., Harrison, P. M. &amp; Gerstein, M. PseudoPipe: an automated pseudogene identification pipeline. Bioinformatics. 22, 1437–1439 (2006)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d27138e3931">65</a></sup> with default parameters. The predicted protein-coding gene sequences from both <i>G. barbadense</i> subgenomes were used as queries to search repeat-masked intergenic regions. Putative pseudogenes were filtered by excluding genes that significantly overlapped with functional gene annotations, genes with parental genes annotated as transposon elements or plastid genes and genes with sequence lengths shorter than 150 bp.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">RNA extraction and transcriptome sequencing</h3><p>The total RNA from each sample was extracted using TRIzol reagent (Invitrogen) following a standard protocol. The mRNAs were purified with the MicroPoly(A) Purist Kit (Ambion), fragmented and converted into an RNA-Seq library using the mRNAseq library construction kit (Illumina Inc.) and sequenced <i>via</i> Illumina Hiseq2000. The mRNAs of 28 samples were also pooled and sequenced on the 454 Genome Sequencer FLX instrument.</p><p>Sequence reads from all samples were cleaned using the FASTX toolkit (<a href="http://hannonlab.cshl.edu/fastx_toolkit/">http://hannonlab.cshl.edu/fastx_toolkit/</a>). All reads containing ‘N’ were discarded. Adapter sequences were then removed using the fastx_clipper program, followed by the removal of low-quality (Q &lt; 5) bases from the 3′ end with fastq_quality_trimmer while requiring a minimum sequence length of 50 bp.</p><p>The RNA-Seq reads of each sample were mapped to the A<sub>t</sub> and D<sub>t</sub> genes using bowtie2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Langmead, B., Trapnell, C., Pop, M. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Ultrafast and memory-efficient alignment of short DNA sequences to the human genome. Genome Biol. 10, R25 (2009)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d27138e3966">66</a></sup> with a mismatch in seed alignment of 0. Differentially expressed genes were identified <i>via</i> the DEGseq package using the MARS method (MA-plot-based method with Random Sampling model)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Wang, L., Feng, Z., Wang, X., Wang, X. &amp; Zhang, X. DEGseq: an R package for identifying differentially expressed genes from RNA-seq data. Bioinformatics. 26, 136–138 (2010)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d27138e3973">67</a></sup> based on their RPKM (Reads Per Kilobases per Million reads) or FPKM (reads per kilobase of exon model per million mapped reads) values<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Mortazavi, A., Williams, B. A., McCue, K., Schaeffer, L. &amp; Wold, B. Mapping and quantifying mammalian transcriptomes by RNA-Seq. Nat Methods. 5, 621–628 (2008)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d27138e3978">68</a></sup> with an FDR≤0.001 and |log<sub>2</sub> Ratio |≥ 1 as the threshold. KEGG pathway enrichment was performed with a corrected P-value of &lt; 0.05 as a threshold. GO enrichment was performed using Blast2go<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Conesa, A., Gotz, S., Garcia-Gomez, J. M., Terol, J., Talon, M. &amp; Robles, M. Blast2GO: a universal tool for annotation, visualization and analysis in functional genomics research. Bioinformatics. 21, 3674–3676 (2005)." href="/articles/srep14139#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d27138e3984">57</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec20" data-title="Additional Information"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec20-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec20">Additional Information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec20-content"><p><b>Accession numbers</b>: The <i>G. barbadense</i> genome assembly contigs and scaffolds have been deposited in GenBank under PRJNA251673. The sequences and functional annotation of <i>G. barbadense</i> protein encoding genes, including predicted genes and transcriptome data, are available from the website. (<a href="http://database.chgc.sh.cn/cotton/index.html">http://database.chgc.sh.cn/cotton/index.html</a>).</p><p><b>How to cite this article</b>: Liu, X. <i>et al.</i> <i>Gossypium barbadense</i> genome sequence provides insight into the evolution of extra-long staple fiber and specialized metabolites. <i>Sci. Rep.</i> <b>5</b>, 14139; doi: 10.1038/srep14139 (2015).</p></div></div></section>

X.L., Y.H., B.Z., L.-J.W., X.-X.S.G., Y.-L.B, D.M., W.-H.L., S.W. and X.-F.Z. collected the samples and extracted genomic DNA and RNA. S.-Y.W., Y.-Q.Z., L.J., H.K., L.-J.W., J.C., S.-T.Y., S.-T.C, B.-Y.W., J.W. and W.-Y.G. performed the genome, transcriptome and BAC clone sequencing. G.L., J.-D.C., D.M. and S.-Y.W. assembled the genome and EST sequence data. W.-Z.G., L.F., C.-P.C., B.-L.Z., Y.-Z.W., L.Z., J.-P.J., Y.-Q.Z., X.-L.H., R.W., S.-C.S., X.-Y.L. and Z.-C.G. performed the gene prediction, annotation and validation. H.-J.Z., B.Z., C.-Q.Y., D.Y.C., J.-X.R., G.L., C.-M.S., Y.-B.M., Y.Z., L.Z., J.-P.J., X.D., X.L., S.-H.X., S.-Y.W., T.-Z.Z., G.-P.Z. and X.-Y.C. performed the genome analysis. X.-Y.C., G.-P.Z., H.-J.Z., C.-Q.Y., B.Z., G.L., Z.J.C., T.-Z.Z., S.-Y.W., Y.Z. and X.L. wrote the manuscript.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="ethics" data-title="Ethics declarations"><div class="c-article-section" id="ethics-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="ethics">Ethics declarations</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="ethics-content">
              
                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading">Competing interests</h3>
                <p>The authors declare no competing financial interests.</p>
